        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adma.202304428')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="ebec1bd5633444eeba2e93a9991cef0a">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'c6b9c63b-db7d-4daf-9892-f7f293cd88c9'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a class="sm-account__link" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:caoxinxin@csu.edu.cn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-ic_mail_solid"></i><span>caoxinxin@csu.edu.cn</span></a></li>
                     </ul>
                     <p>School of Materials Science and Engineering, Central South University, Changsha, Hunan, 410083 P. R. China</p>
                     <p>Key Laboratory of Electronic Packaging and Advanced Functional Materials of Hunan Province, Changsha, Hunan, 410083 P. R. China</p>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d106366580" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Na-superionic-conductor (NASICON)-type cathodes (e.g., Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>) have attracted extensive attention due to their open and robust framework, fast Na<sup>+</sup> mobility, and superior thermal stability. To commercialize sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), higher energy density and lower cost requirements are urgently needed for NASICON-type cathodes. Herein, Na<sub>3.5</sub>V<sub>1.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVFP) is designed by an Fe-substitution strategy, which not only reduces the exorbitant cost of vanadium, but also realizes high-voltage multielectron reactions. The NVFP cathode can deliver extraordinary capacity (148.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and decent cycling durability up to 84% after 10&nbsp;000 cycles at 100 C. In situ X-ray diffraction and ex situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy characterizations reveal reversible structural evolution and redox processes (Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>, V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup>, and V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup>) during electrochemical reactions. The low ionic-migration energy barrier and ideal Na<sup>+</sup>-diffusion kinetics are elucidated by density functional theory calculations. Combined with electron paramagnetic resonance spectroscopy, Fe with unpaired electrons in the 3d orbital is inseparable from the higher-valence redox activation. More competitively, coupling with a hard carbon (HC) anode, HC//NVFP full cells demonstrate high-rate capability and long-duration cycling lifespan (3000&nbsp;stable cycles at 50 C), along with material-level energy density up to 304&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>. The present work can provide new perspectives to accelerate the commercialization of SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202304428-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202304428-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The development of large-scale energy-storage systems (EESs) has become an urgent demand for green energy transformation and carbon neutralization. SIBs are considered as the most promising candidates for large-scale electrical EESs due to their wide availability and low cost.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0001_R_d106366567e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy Nat. Rev. Mater. Nat. Energy Nat. Commun.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, considerable efforts are still in demand to develop new electrode materials with cost-effective, high specific energy and long cycling stability, especially for cathode materials, to achieve satisfactory comprehensive performance in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0002_R_d106366567e481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Commun.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To date, various types of cathode materials have been reported, including layered/tunnel transition metal oxides, polyanionic compounds, and Prussian blue analogues. Polyanionic compounds with strong binding energy of M─O bond (M = P, B, C, Si, etc.) and open framework structures are considered as one of the most promising candidate materials in recent years.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0001_R_d106366567e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy Nat. Rev. Mater. Nat. Energy Nat. Commun.">1</a>-<a href="#adma202304428-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0003_R_d106366567e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Phosphates with NASICON structure have attracted much attention due to their stable 3D frameworks, good thermal stability, and fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0004_R_d106366567e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Commun. Chem. Mater. Adv. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Soc. Rev.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP), as the representative NASICON-type cathode material, has a voltage platform of ≈3.4&nbsp;V, which can deliver a reversible discharge capacity of about 110&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and achieve an energy density of about 370&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0005_R_d106366567e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater. Small">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Low cost (sustainable) and high energy density are two important conditions for the large-scale applications of SIBs in the commercial market. Although many commercialization attempts of NVP have been conducted, the high cost and toxicity of V element remain a critical issue in real applications. In consequence, regulating the redox center of NVP with benign and inexpensive active elements, such as Fe and Mn, is the thoughtful choice to achieve more cost-effective cathodes without significant capacity loss. Meanwhile, the redox couples of V in this structure are V<sup>2+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup>, V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup>, and V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup>, corresponding to potentials of about 1.6, 3.4, and 4.0&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0006_R_d106366567e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - InfoMat">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is desirable to activate more than two electrons to participate in the redox processes (such as V3<sup>+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> redox couples) to obtain higher specific capacity and operating voltage. With these considerations in mind, Goodenough et&nbsp;al. first reported Na<sub>4</sub>VMn(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> with an extra higher voltage platform of Mn<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> (3.6&nbsp;V) by introducing low-cost and less-toxic Mn.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0007_R_d106366567e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano Lett.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, structural degradation due to the intrinsic Jahn–Teller distortion of Mn<sup>3+</sup> in Mn-based materials will lead to unsatisfactory performance and irreversible structural evolution at higher charging voltages, even if the V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> redox couple can be achieved (with three-electron transfer).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0008_R_d106366567e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Small Methods">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Jin et&nbsp;al. concluded that introducing Cr<sup>3+</sup> into Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> can stimulate V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> (≈4.0&nbsp;V) active redox reaction, and then combining low voltage V<sup>2+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup> (≈1.6&nbsp;V) redox couple can achieve a reversible three-electron reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0009_R_d106366567e596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, the widely spaced voltage platforms affect its practical applications. Masquelier et&nbsp;al. developed a Na<sub>4</sub>VFe(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode by introduction of low-cost Fe, which can realize about 2.76-electron redox reaction through Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>, V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup>, and V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> redox couples.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0010_R_d106366567e621" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> But there is still a large gap between the actual electrochemical properties and the theoretical value. It follows that the development of a NASICON-type cathode with stable structure, multielectron reaction, high working voltage, and cost-effective way is key current issue and challenge, and further explorations are still urgently needed.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, a high-abundance and low-cost Fe dopant was employed for substitution V element in NASICON-type Na<sub>3+</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>V<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Fe<i><sub>x</sub></i>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0001">1a</a>). Our findings demonstrate that introducing Fe at the V site not only achieves a new plateau for reversible Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> redox couple besides the original V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> center, but also activates the V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> redox (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0001">1b</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0011_R_d106366567e667" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater. Carbon">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a result, reversible multielectron redox reactions of Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>, V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup>, and V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> redox couples have been achieved, resulting in remarkable improvement in energy density (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0001">1c</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0012_R_d106366567e689" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The NVFP electrode exhibits excellent electrochemical performance, delivering an impressive discharge capacity of 148.2&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 C and achieving a remarkable energy density of 501&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the mass of cathode material). More importantly, in terms of application viability, the HC//NVFP full cells demonstrate superior cycling stability (3000 stable cycles at 50 C), and a material-level energy density of 304&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> that surpasses other NASICON-type counterparts. In situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) and ex situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analyses further reveal reversible structural evolution and variations of oxidation states during the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion process of NVFP in detail. Density functional theory (DFT) calculations clarify the high redox activity, favorable electronic structure, ideal Na<sup>+</sup> migration paths and barriers of NASICON-type Na<sub>3+</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>V<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Fe<i><sub>x</sub></i>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> with Fe substitution. In combination with electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) spectra, it is further demonstrated that Fe with unpaired electrons in 3d orbital plays a crucial role in the activation of the higher-voltage redox couple. Solid-state nuclear magnetic resonance (ss-NMR) and Rietveld XRD results indicate that the inclusion of Fe also impacts the structural rearrangement of Na sites, leading to additional preferential occupation of active Na2 sites by Na<sup>+</sup>, thereby providing available active Na<sup>+</sup> for V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> redox reaction. Our current findings provide a profound insight into the multielectron redox chemistry in V-based NASICON-type phosphate cathodes, resulting in an improvement of overall capacity and voltage output, which further promotes the development of low-cost, high-energy-density SIBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202304428-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d55228bd-19d5-4bc2-adec-2ab19e89f475/adma202304428-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d55228bd-19d5-4bc2-adec-2ab19e89f475/adma202304428-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/01d7daaf-b230-4343-b891-e396f6ed4640/adma202304428-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d55228bd-19d5-4bc2-adec-2ab19e89f475/adma202304428-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Theoretical and electrochemical results of the NASICON-type Na<sub>3+</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>V<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Fe<i><sub>x</sub></i>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> materials. a) Schematic illustration of the crystal structure. b) Design of a mechanism to activate multielectron redox chemistry. c) The calculated voltage profile for Na<sub>3.5</sub>V<sub>1.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVFP). The dashed line is considered inaccessible voltage according to the phase stability analysis. d–f) Total DOS and the projected element DOS of NVP (d), NVFP (e), and NVFP-1 (f) materials based on the DFT calculations. g) Intuitionistic comparison of second charge–discharge profiles and Coulombic efficiencies. h) The preliminary cycling performance of three electrodes at 1 C. i) Capacity versus voltage plot of this work and other reported NASICON-type Na-storage cathode materials.</div>
