         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202304428-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202304428-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The evolution of the electronic structures upon the tuning of V/Fe ratio in NASICON-type Na<sub>3+</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>V<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Fe<i><sub>x</sub></i>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> is verified by DFT calculations. The optimized crystal structures of NVP, NVFP, and Na<sub>4</sub>VFe(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVFP-1) are depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), and their corresponding density of states (DOS) are revealed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0001">1d–f</a>. The valence band and conduction band of the three materials are composed of hybrid V 3d, P 2p, and O 1p orbitals, respectively, among which Fe 3d orbital can also be observed in NVFP and NVFP-1. It is clear from their total DOS graphs that the forbidden bandgap of NVFP and NVFP-1 is reduced to 1.05 and 1.04&nbsp;eV, respectively, indicating that the electronic conductivity of NVFP and NVFP-1 is higher than that of NVP, which can be attributed to the hybridized Fe 3d orbital. Moreover, NVFP and NVFP-1 show more generous electron distribution near the Fermi level, which confirms that they have higher redox activity in their respective electrochemical reaction process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0013_R_d106366567e814" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This will be verified in subsequent experimental results.</p>
         
         <p>The NASICON-type Na<sub>3+</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>V<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Fe<i><sub>x</sub></i>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode materials were synthesized via a facile one-step solid-state method followed by the calcination process under argon flow. The detailed synthesis procedures for both samples can be found in the Experimental Section (Supporting Information). The XRD results of NVP, NVFP, and NVFP-1 confirm that the three materials have high purity and good crystallinity (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). With the increase of Fe substitution, the diffraction peaks exhibit a constant shift to lower angles, indicating the successful incorporation and combination of Na<sup>+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup> into the original crystal structure. However, when half of the V is replaced, impurity peaks attributed to maricite NaFePO<sub>4</sub> can be detected in the range of 30–36°.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0014_R_d106366567e848" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical properties of NVP, NVFP, and NVFP-1 were tested in coin cells with Na metal anodes. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0001">1g</a> exhibits the second charge–discharge profiles of three electrodes at 0.5 C between 1.7 and 4.3&nbsp;V. Three electrodes all show obvious voltage plateaus, and the new high-voltage plateaus are stimulated by the addition of Fe. These pronounced plateaus correspond to redox couples of Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> (≈2.5&nbsp;V), V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> (≈3.4&nbsp;V), and V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> (≈4.0&nbsp;V), which indicates that the emergence of new V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> plateau enables NVFP and NVFP-1 electrodes to provide higher capacity. Moreover, the coulombic efficiency of NVFP (97%) is higher than that of NVFP-1 (94%). With the increase of Fe proportion, the capacity contribution of the high voltage platform is also increased, and the charge/discharge profiles of NVFP and NVFP-1 are also more skewed. It has been reported that element doping in Na layered oxides can smooth charge/discharge profiles and inhibit phase transitions, ultimately improving electrochemical properties, but this is not expected in phosphate compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0015_R_d106366567e875" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Energy Lett.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the redox peaks corresponding to charge/discharge plateaus can be observed in the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3d–f</a>, Supporting Information), and the redox peaks of V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> gradually decrease and widen, which is mainly ascribed to the reduction of V and the increase of covalent characteristics after Fe<sup>2+</sup> substitution, which can also be confirmed by d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> plots (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a–c</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0015_R_d106366567e898" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Energy Lett.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The comparison of cycling performance further gives a prominence to the superiority of NVFP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0001">1h</a>). The NVFP electrode demonstrates superior cycling stability with a capacity retention of 92% after 100 cycles at 1 C. NVP and NVFP-1 electrodes show rapid capacity degradation, with capacity retentions of only 50.7%, and 28.8%, respectively. In the case of cathode materials, specific capacity and operating voltage are both critical. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information), when NVFP is matched with a HC anode, its energy density (depending on voltage and capacity) is the highest among the three cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0016_R_d106366567e910" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Res.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0001">1i</a>, NVFP electrode exhibits excellent energy density and power density simply based on the mass of the cathode material. The energy density of NVFP cathode is ≈501&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> calculated in the voltage range of 1.7–4.3&nbsp;V at 0.5&nbsp;C, which far surpasses the well-known Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (≈386&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>) and Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (≈378&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>) cathodes, and is superior to most reported NASICON-type cathode materials for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0005_R_d106366567e946" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater. Small">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, NVFP was selected for further intensive research.</p>
         
         <p>The XRD Rietveld refinement result of NVFP material (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0002">2a</a>) indicates the presence of a phase-pure assigned to the trigonal NASICON structure with a space group of <i>R-3c</i>, which has a good weighted profile R factor (<i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> = 4.0%). The crystal structure of NVFP consists of a 3D open framework connecting the MO<sub>6</sub> (M = Fe, V) octahedron and PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedron in a corner-shared way, which creates favorable channels for Na<sup>+</sup> migration and provides two independent sites for Na<sup>+</sup> ((Na1, 6b) and (Na2, 18e)) depending on the oxygen coordination environment (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0001">1a</a>). The structure details on lattice parameters, atomic coordinates and occupancy are given in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1 and S2</a> (Supporting Information). Fe<sup>2+</sup> has a high spin state in the phosphate framework of NVFP, so the radius of Fe<sup>2+</sup> (0.78 Å) is larger than that of V<sup>3+</sup> (0.64 Å) and resulting in the increase of unit cell volume. In addition, the contraction along the <i>c</i>-direction is mainly due to the addition of extra Na<sup>+</sup>, which leads to the increase of Na1 site occupation and the decrease of the electrostatic repulsion of MO<sub>6</sub> (M = Fe, V) octahedron along the <i>c</i>-axis.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0011_R_d106366567e996" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater. Carbon">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Raman spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0002">2b</a>) clearly reveals two characteristic carbon peaks of the NVFP, corresponding to D-band (1350 cm<sup>−1</sup>) and G-band (1590 cm<sup>−1</sup>), respectively. The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value is 1.09, suggestive of a partial graphitization of the carbon skeleton, resulting in an improved electronic conductivity. In addition, the peaks located at around 431 and 993 cm<sup>−1</sup> can be indexed to stretching vibrations of [PO<sub>4</sub>] groups.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0017_R_d106366567e1019" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Storage Mater. Small">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The in situ carbon coating obtained from oleic acid pyrolysis during calcination can effectively improve the electronic conductivity of the materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0018_R_d106366567e1025" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The typical vibration modes of both the PO<sub>4</sub> and MO<sub>6</sub> units were also detected in the Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectrum (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202304428-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/69898c8c-8122-4daa-b8aa-8439e983012d/adma202304428-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/69898c8c-8122-4daa-b8aa-8439e983012d/adma202304428-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e803f92e-8a68-4b0b-9f22-8f855e4163f6/adma202304428-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/69898c8c-8122-4daa-b8aa-8439e983012d/adma202304428-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304428-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304428" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural characterizations of the NVFP material. a) XRD Rietveld refinement plots of NVFP. b) Raman scattering spectrum. c) Nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms and corresponding pore size distribution curve (inset). d,e) High-resolution V 2p (d) and Fe 2p (e) XPS spectra. f) TEM image and g) HRTEM image with the corresponding FFT pattern (inset). h) HAADF-STEM image and corresponding elemental mapping images.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The two peaks near 540 and 630 cm<sup>−1</sup> may be the elastic vibration of M─O (M = V, Fe) bonds. Meanwhile, the bands located at 600 and 1200 cm<sup>−1</sup> are responsible for the PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedron, while the band in the range of 1170 and 1200 cm<sup>−1</sup> can be traced to typical stretching vibration of PO<sub>4</sub> units.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0015_R_d106366567e1071" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Energy Lett.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thermogravimetric (TG) analysis shows the carbon content of NVFP is about 12.5&nbsp;wt% in removing the effect of adsorptive water (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). The specific surface area and pore size distribution of the sample were investigated by nitrogen isothermal adsorption-desorption technique. The NVFP material shows the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area of 25.27 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> with a uniform mesoporous distribution about 3.9&nbsp;nm, which facilitates electrolyte penetration and full capacity delivery of electrodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0002">2c</a>).</p>
         
         <p>The XPS was used to preliminarily detect NVFP material, and the chemical valence states of V and Fe are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0002">2d,e</a>. Both the 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and 2p<sub>2/3</sub> orbitals of V and Fe can be measured and deconvolved. The peaks at 516.9 (V 2p<sub>1/2</sub>) and 523.5&nbsp;eV (V 2p<sub>3/2</sub>) of V 2p spectrum are characteristic peaks of trivalent V species, while the binding energies of divalent and trivalent Fe appear simultaneously, indicating the slight oxidation and the mixed valence state coexistence of Fe in NVFP material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0010_R_d106366567e1101" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Mater.">10</a>-<a href="#adma202304428-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0019_R_d106366567e1101" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Storage Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The XPS survey spectrum and high-resolution XPS spectra of other identified elements are displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information). The C 1s spectrum can be decomposed into four kinds of carbon peaks, assigned to C─C, C─O, C═O, and O─C═O bonds, respectively. The high ratio of sp<sup>2</sup> C─C bond indicates that the carbon coating layer on NVFP particles has good electronic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0013_R_d106366567e1111" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The typical scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information) visually confirm the irregular stacked nanoparticles morphology with dispersed pores of the as-prepared NVFP material. It can be clearly observed from transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0002">2f,g</a>, the nanoparticles ranging from 100 to 300&nbsp;nm of NVFP are effectively and uniformly encapsulated in the continuous carbon matrix with a thickness of 5&nbsp;nm. In the HRTEM image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0002">2g</a>), the well-defined lattice fringes with <i>d</i>-spacings of 6.2 Å, corresponds to the (012) plane in the NVFP lattice, also confirmed by the corresponding fast Fourier transform (FFT) pattern (the inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0002">2g</a>). Furthermore, the high-angle annular dark-field scanning TEM image (HAADF-STEM) and corresponding elemental mapping images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0002">2h</a>) demonstrate that Na, Fe, V, P, O, and C are homogeneously distributed throughout the as-prepared NVFP particles.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical properties of the NVFP cathode were evaluated in coin-type half cells with sodium metal anodes. First, the charge–discharge profiles of NVFP were measured and compared in different voltage windows. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0003">3a</a>, under a voltage range of 1.7–3.8&nbsp;V, two distinct voltage plateaus appear in the galvanostatic profile of NVFP at about 2.55 and 3.46&nbsp;V, corresponding to the two-electron redox reactions of Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> and V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup>, respectively. The corresponding specific capacity can reach ≈110&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> (similar to the capacity of NVP). When the charge cut-off voltage increases to 4.3&nbsp;V, a new voltage plateau appears around 4&nbsp;V, probably due to partial activation of the V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> redox center. As a result, the NVFP electrode delivers an extraordinary capacity of 148.2&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> (equivalent to removal of ≈2.5 Na<sup>+</sup> per formula unit) and can be able to achieve higher energy densities as the charging cut-off voltage increases, which will be discussed later.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202304428-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/dcb0a0ab-a71d-40cb-b3df-80168658f789/adma202304428-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/dcb0a0ab-a71d-40cb-b3df-80168658f789/adma202304428-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/da287a49-5fe8-4848-9020-7f7a32eb12ec/adma202304428-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/dcb0a0ab-a71d-40cb-b3df-80168658f789/adma202304428-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304428-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304428" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties of as-prepared NVFP in the voltage window of 2–3.8&nbsp;V. a) The galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles at 0.5 C are compared in different voltage windows of 1.7–3.8 and 1.7–4.3&nbsp;V. b) Charge–discharge profiles and c) rate capability from 0.5 to 100 C (1&nbsp;C&nbsp;= 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). d,e) Long-term cycling stability at 1 C and 5 C (d) and at 100 C (e). f) CV curves at various scan rates from 0.1 to 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. g) Log(<i>v</i>) vs log(<i>i</i>) plots for the different peaks in the CV curves, where <i>i</i> is the peak current and <i>v</i> is the scan rate. h) GITT curves and the corresponding diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> at different charge and discharge states.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The electrodes were charged/discharged under two different voltage windows of 2–3.8 and 1.7–4.3&nbsp;V, respectively. Under the voltage window of 2–3.8&nbsp;V, the rate performance is revealed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0003">3b,c</a>. The discharge curves of NVFP at different rates can be clearly detected, and it can deliver discharge capacity of 112&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5&nbsp;C. With the increase of C-rates, the discharge plateaus of NVFP electrode decrease, but it still exhibits excellent rate performance, expressing a reversible capacity of 68&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> even at a high rate of 100 C. The extended cycling performance at different rates also expresses remarkable superiority. The capacity of the NVFP electrode decreases less and remains at ≈105&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> during 1000 cycles at 1 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0003">3d</a>). Furthermore, the initial discharge capacity is 104.0&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C, while 96.0&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> can still be obtained after 1000 cycles, with a retention rate of 92% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0003">3d</a>). As seen, the NVFP electrode shows inhibition of voltage attenuation with an outstanding capacity retention at both 1 and 5 C for 1000 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). More impressively, even at 100 C, the NVFP electrode displays a capacity of 78.7&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 84% after 10&nbsp;000 cycles, illustrating its great promise for real applications in fast-charging and long-life SIBs (Figure <a href="#adma202304428-fig-0003">3e</a>). CV curves measured at different scanning rates and the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) were used to estimate the electrode dynamics of NVFP in the voltage range of 2–3.8&nbsp;V. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0003">3f</a> displays CV curves at various scanning rates of 0.1–1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, and A1/C1 and A2/C2 redox peaks correspond to redox couples of Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> and V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup>, respectively. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0003">3g</a> shows the correlations between the peak current (<i>I</i><sub>p</sub>) and square root of scan rate (<i>ν</i><sup>1/2</sup>) from CV profiles, which fit a linear relationship nicely. The linear dependence indicates that the electrochemical behavior of Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion is a diffusion-controlled process. Therefore, we can use the Randles–Sevcik equation to calculate the apparent diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> (<i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub>) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). According to the calculation, the obtained <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> values can reach the 10<sup>−10</sup>–10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> orders of magnitude, corresponding to the redox peaks of A1, A2, C1, and C2. The NVFP electrode was again verified by GITT to have favorable Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0003">3h</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). The <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> of NVFP electrode varies from 10<sup>−11</sup> to 10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, and the <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> value of Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> redox process is slightly higher than that of V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> redox.</p>
         
         <p>In accordance with the charge–discharge profiles in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0003">3a</a>, when the charge cut-off voltage is increased to 4.3&nbsp;V, the high-voltage redox couple V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> may be activated and participate in the reactions. As can be seen from the rate charge–discharge profiles, with the increase of the rate (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0004">4a</a>), the overall trend of the curve is consistent with the narrow voltage window (2–3.8&nbsp;V), but due to the emergence of a new voltage plateau, the electrode capacity gets an additional increase. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0004">4b</a>, NVFP electrode exhibits a capacity of 148.2&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 C, which is equivalent to an additional 0.5 electron transfer compared to the capacity at the narrow voltage window. Even at the extremely high rate of 100 C, the specific capacity still remains 73.5&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, and when the current density falls back to 0.5 C, the capacity recovers to 145.2&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, which reflects the stability of the crystal structure. In the Ragone plots (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0004">4c</a>), although the median voltage of NVFP is only 3.38&nbsp;V at 0.5 C, its calculated energy density is about 501&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage window of 1.7–4.3&nbsp;V, which has surpassed NVP and outperformed most of V-based, Fe-based, and other reported phosphate cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0002_R_d106366567e1339" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Commun.">2</a>, <a href="#adma202304428-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0015_R_d106366567e1339" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Energy Lett.">15</a>, <a href="#adma202304428-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0017_R_d106366567e1339" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Storage Mater. Small">17</a>, <a href="#adma202304428-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0019_R_d106366567e1339" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Storage Mater.">19</a>, <a href="#adma202304428-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0020_R_d106366567e1339" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Chem. Eng. J. Adv. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202304428-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/99873fb2-b34d-4572-bddd-0e794f35326b/adma202304428-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/99873fb2-b34d-4572-bddd-0e794f35326b/adma202304428-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e0c0787e-4ee1-4076-82c9-6e3d10ff2811/adma202304428-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/99873fb2-b34d-4572-bddd-0e794f35326b/adma202304428-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304428-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304428" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties of as-prepared NVFP in the voltage window of 1.7–4.3&nbsp;V. a) Charge–discharge profiles and b) rate capability from 0.5 to 100 C. c) Ragone plots for comparison between NVFP and other reported phosphate cathodes. d,e) Long-term cycling stability at 1 C and 5 C (d), and at 100 C (e). f) CV curves at various scan rates from 0.1 to 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. g) Log(<i>v</i>) vs log(<i>i</i>) plots for the different peaks in the CV curves. h) GITT curves and the corresponding diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> during the charge and discharge processes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In the voltage windows of 1.7–4.3&nbsp;V, the cycling performance at 1 and 5 C is illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0004">4d</a>. NVFP electrode shows suppressed voltage decay (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a</a>, Supporting Information), prospective capacity (≈141&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>), and excellent capacity retention after 280 cycles at 1 C. After 500 cycles at 5 C, a reversible capacity of 92.2&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> is obtained, and the loss rate of single cycle capacity is only 0.056% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12b</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the NVFP can deliver an initial discharge capacity of 89.8&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at the high rate of 100 C and withstand 10&nbsp;000 long-term cycles durably (Figure 4e). By comparing the cycling performance under different voltage windows, it can be found that the cycling stability decreases when the charge cut-off voltage is increased, which may be related to the instability of electrolyte under high voltage. Therefore, the LSV curve of the used electrolyte (1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF6 in PC with 5% FEC) was measured (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). The decomposition of electrolyte is observed at the voltage higher than 4&nbsp;V. Therefore, optimizing the electrolyte system is a crucial aspect to boost the multielectron redox chemistry in NASICON-type NVFP. It can be clearly seen from the CV curves that three different redox peaks correspond to Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>, V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup>, and V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> redox couples, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0004">4f</a>). Moreover, the calculated b values strongly prove that diffusion and pseudocapacitance controlled processes coexist during the charge and discharge processes, and both of them have a positive effect on the overall electrochemical performance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0004">4g</a>). The <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> values are characterized based on GITT (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0004">4h</a>). Compared to the situation at low voltage, the <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> of V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> oxidation process decreases during the charging process, resulting in an obvious overpotential that indicates sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. This observation is consistent with the <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> values as calculated by Randles–Sevcik equation for CV curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>In order to obtain a more comprehensive understanding of the structural superiority of the NVFP electrode under different voltage windows, in situ XRD patterns during the second (2-3.8&nbsp;V) and the third (1.7-4.3&nbsp;V) charge/discharge process are recorded to illustrate the structural evolution (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0005">5a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). As charging proceeds to the voltage of 2.75&nbsp;V, all the XRD peaks shift to higher angles, indicating that the lattice volume shrinks after the gradual release of Na<sup>+</sup>, which is consistent with the solid-solution reaction mechanism.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0011_R_d106366567e1452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater. Carbon">11</a>, <a href="#adma202304428-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0021_R_d106366567e1452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This stage corresponds to the Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> redox couple (region I), and the electrode undergoes a topotactic one-phase transition from Na<sub>3.5</sub>V<sub>1.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> to Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. When the voltage exceeds 2.75&nbsp;V, the new diffraction peak of (104) gradually separates and the disappearance/enhancement of (211)/(116) reflection peaks indicates the emergence of a new phase, which involves a typical biphasic transition mechanism. It can be seen that in the voltage range of 2.75–3.8&nbsp;V, correspondingly, cathode electrode changes from Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> phase to Na<sub>1.5</sub>V<sub>1.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> phase, with the processive oxidation of V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> (region II). Moreover, as charging further proceeds to 4.3&nbsp;V, the major peaks show a continuous shift toward higher angles, demonstrating the lattice has experienced unprecedented shrinkage accompanied by continuous extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>, which is attributed to partial oxidation of V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> (region III), resulting in the final Na-poor phase NaV<sub>1.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0011_R_d106366567e1525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater. Carbon">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, with the re-insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>, regardless of the narrow or wide voltage windows, the new peaks gradually disappear, and major peaks return to their original values almost in a symmetric path, indicating that the robust crystal framework can be well maintained. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0005">5b</a>, cell parameters are calculated by refinement of XRD from selected stages during reversible lattice breathing. All three lattice parameters have changed reversibly, corresponding to the variation in the detected peaks as well. It is worth noting that the electrode experienced a tiny volume change of about 7.2%, which is smaller than most NASICON-type cathodes for SIBs (volume change normalized to per electron transfer) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). In general, all these peak evolution characteristics reveal the reversible phase transition during the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion of the NVFP electrode, which should be responsible for its excellent cycling stability. In addition, the change of elemental valence state of NVFP electrode during electrochemical reactions was further investigated by ex situ XPS. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0005">5c</a>, XPS spectra of Fe 2p and V 2p under different charging states were collected. In the initial state, NVFP electrode exhibits the basic characteristics of Fe<sup>2+</sup> and V<sup>3+</sup>. Upon charging to 3.8&nbsp;V, the XPS peak of V2p develops toward high binding energy, indicating that V<sup>3+</sup> is completely oxidized to V<sup>4+</sup> valence state, and Fe undergoes the same transition (Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0022_R_d106366567e1557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Energy Adv. Sci.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, charge transfer at narrow voltage window is entirely contributed by Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> and V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> redox couples. Upon further charging to 4.3&nbsp;V, Fe binding energy remains at +3 valence with no significant difference, while according to the change of binding energy of V, it is confirmed that part of V<sup>4+</sup> is oxidized to V<sup>5+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0023_R_d106366567e1576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Energy Storage Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Then, the XPS spectra of V2p and Fe2p return to their original state following discharging to 1.7&nbsp;V, which demonstrates the reversible chemical states of V and Fe in the whole process of Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202304428-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e169ceab-20af-475f-910b-f85e4589e6bb/adma202304428-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e169ceab-20af-475f-910b-f85e4589e6bb/adma202304428-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5b2da6da-641a-4847-b0fc-939bb7fa6cc1/adma202304428-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e169ceab-20af-475f-910b-f85e4589e6bb/adma202304428-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304428-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304428" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Na-storage mechanism and Na<sup>+</sup> dynamics investigations. a) In situ XRD 2D contour plot and corresponding charge–discharge curves of the NVFP electrode during the second (2–3.8&nbsp;V) and the third (1.7–4.3&nbsp;V) charge/discharge process. b) Lattice parameter variations at different cell potentials. c) High-resolution XPS spectra of Fe and V elements for NVFP electrode at different electrochemical state. d) Formation energy (convex hull) of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>V<sub>1.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.5, 1, 1.5, 2, 2.5, 3, 3.5, 4) from the DFT calculations. e) Schematic illustration of the possible migration pathways represented by the yellow isosurface in the primitive cell model of NVFP. f) Calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion pathways and the corresponding migration energy barriers within the different Na<sup>+</sup> groups. g) The comparative electronic spin states and h) EPR spectra for NVFP and NVP.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In order to further understand the structural changes of Na<sub>x</sub>V<sub>1.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> during Na<sup>+</sup> extraction, the formation energies of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>V<sub>1.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> structures with <i>x</i> = 0–4 were calculated by DFT (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0005">5d</a>). It found that all Na<sup>+</sup> in a single unit cell can be divided into different types according to their individual binding energies, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information). According to the formula <i>E</i><sub>form</sub> = <i>E</i><sub>Nax</sub> − 0.3334[(<i>x</i> − 1) <i>E</i><sub>Na4</sub>] − 0.3334[(4 − <i>x</i>) <i>E</i><sub>Na1</sub>, the convex hull phase diagram of the per formula unit (<i>E</i><sub>form</sub>) with Na<sub>0</sub>V<sub>1.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>4</sub>V<sub>1.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as endmembers is created, where the calculation step of <i>x</i> is set as 0.5, and the specific calculation data are shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0024_R_d106366567e1709" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Energy Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The calculation results indicate that Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> is the most stable thermodynamic phase in the end-member configuration, and the other intermediate phases from <i>x</i> = 1–4 are metastable phases, suggesting the multielectron redox feasibility of this NASICON-type material. Besides, the sequential reactions for each metastable phase are expected to occur at 4.2&nbsp;V (V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup>), 3.6&nbsp;V (V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup>), and 2.8&nbsp;V (Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>), respectively, according to the lowest energy structures (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0001">1c</a>), which are very close to the experimental measurements. According to previous studies, bond valence (BV) could obtain the ion transport pathways visually and effectively verify the ion transport mechanism. Therefore, the possible Na<sup>+</sup> migration pathways of NVP, NVFP, and NVFP-1 were obtained by Bond Valance Energy Landscape (BVEL).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0008_R_d106366567e1746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Small Methods">8</a>, <a href="#adma202304428-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0022_R_d106366567e1746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Energy Adv. Sci.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0005">5e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information), it is clearly observed that NVFP shows widened Na<sup>+</sup> migration channels near the FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedron compared with NVP, and NVFP-1 also reveals the same phenomenon, indicating that Fe doping leads to its excellent localized Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics. Through the Na<sup>+</sup> migration path simulated by BVEL method, we reasonably construct four possible migration paths: Na1→Na1 (P1), Na→Na2 (P2), Na2→Na1 (P3), and Na2→Na2 (P4). Based on the climbing-image nudged elastic band (cNEB) method, the corresponding migration energy barriers of P1, P2, P3, and P4 paths are calculated to be 3.82, 1.49, 0.77, and 2.89&nbsp;eV, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0005">5f</a>). Therefore, the most favorable diffusion pathway for Na<sup>+</sup> in the NVFP structure is the Na2→Na1 mode. The low diffusion energy barrier is the reason for the outstanding rate performance of this material.</p>
         
         <p>The electron spin states obtained by DFT calculation can confirm that Fe doping with unpaired electrons changes the spin magnetic moment of 3d orbitals of MO<sub>6</sub> (M = V and Fe) octahedron, resulting in the formation of asymmetric spin configuration of NVFP and the activation of residual valence electrons, which enables the redox of V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> couple (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0005">5g</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0025_R_d106366567e1783" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is well known that EPR is a powerful tool for accurately detecting unpaired electronic information on the surface and bulk of the material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0026_R_d106366567e1789" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To further demonstrate the asymmetric electron configuration induced by Fe doping, EPR spectroscopy was conducted (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0005">5h</a>). It turns out that the NVP has no signal response because V<sup>3+</sup> with electron configuration t<sub>2g</sub><sup>2</sup>e<sub>g</sub><sup>0</sup> (<i>S</i> = 1) is a non-Kramer's ion and is “EPR silent” under conventional perpendicular polarization mode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0027_R_d106366567e1808" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Phys. Chem. C">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, NVFP with iron doping shows a clear pair of EPR signals, but since Fe<sup>2+</sup> also has no EPR response, this could be attributed to the high spin Fe<sup>3+</sup> (d5, <i>S</i> = 5/2). In the Raman spectra, the symmetric bending of the PO<sub>4</sub> group (<i>δ</i><sub>s</sub>) and the vibrational vibrations of the M-ion (<i>υ</i>) show upper field shift trend, which may be a consequence of the higher spin polarization caused by the unpaired 3d electrons (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19a</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0028_R_d106366567e1831" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Phys. Chem. Solids">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> ss-NMR is designed to probe the ionic dynamics, local structural environments, and electronic structure of nuclear spin-based electrodes exposed to an external magnetic field.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0029_R_d106366567e1836" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To unravel insight into the structural effects of unpaired electrons, the <sup>23</sup>Na ss-NMR spectrum of the NVFP electrode was collected and analyzed (the spinning sideband, SSB), as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19b</a> (Supporting Information). Compared with previous reports, the full width is narrower (600&nbsp;ppm, covering all SSBs), which may be due to the relatively small proportion of Fe, so the electron–nuclear dipolar coupling caused by unpaired electrons is changed compared with previous ones.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0011_R_d106366567e1847" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater. Carbon">11</a>, <a href="#adma202304428-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0019_R_d106366567e1847" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Storage Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, two obvious isotopic peaks of Na2 (0.5&nbsp;ppm) and Na1 (−9.4&nbsp;ppm) can also be observed, and the small peak at −20.6&nbsp;ppm can be assigned to Na1'. It can be seen obviously that the integral area of Na2 is higher than that of Na1, which is in line with the results obtained by the Rietveld XRD. The incorporation of Fe also affects the structural rearrangement of Na site.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0030_R_d106366567e1852" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The additional Na<sup>+</sup> preferentially occupies the active Na2 site and provides available active Na<sup>+</sup> for V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> redox reaction, which is essential for the reversible activation of V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> redox couple at the higher-voltage region. The above results indicate that the introduction of specific transition metals with unpaired 3d orbital electrons has a profound effect on the Na diffusion kinetics, electronic structure, as well as the activation of the higher-voltage redox couple.</p>
         
         <p>The feasibility for the practical application of SIBs was evaluated by full-cell devices. The sodium-ion full cells (SIFCs) are assembled with NVFP cathode and commercial HC anode, denoted as HC//NVFP, and their working principle is shown in inset in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0006">6a</a>. The electrochemical properties of HC in the voltage range of 0.01–3&nbsp;V are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> (Supporting Information). The charge–discharge profiles of NVFP cathode, HC anode and HC//NVFP full cell are also presented (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0006">6a</a>). As can be seen, the HC//NVFP SIFCs possess nearly identical voltage profiles as the NVFP cathode in half-cell, while the voltage plateaus merely drop about 0.05–0.2&nbsp;V due to the low sodiation/desodiation voltages of hard carbon, revealing the high feasibility in respect of both high-voltage plateaus and high energy density in the HC//NVFP SIFCs. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0006">6b</a>, when the rates of 0.5–100 C are applied to HC//NVFP SIFCs, the charge-discharge profiles can keep the same shape no matter in narrow voltage window (1.5–3.6&nbsp;V) or wide voltage window (1.5–4.1&nbsp;V), indicating its highly reversible Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion process. The delivered discharge capacity of HC//NVFP SIFCs in the voltage window of 1.5–3.6&nbsp;V can still remain as high as 63.2&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> even at a high rate of 100 C, which is about 71.5% of capacity at 0.5 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0006">6c</a>). Moreover, the SIFCs with extraordinary rate performance are demonstrated in the voltage interval of 1.5–4.1&nbsp;V, providing rate capacities of 166.6, 143.9, 127.8, 113.7, 105, 95.3, 81.1, and 68.9&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, at 0.5, 1, 2, 5, 10, 20, 50, and 100 C, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0006">6c</a>). In order to further implement the durability of their application, the cycling stability of the HC//NVFP SIFCs was investigated. The SIFCs deliver an initial capacity of 80.3&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage window of 1.5–3.6&nbsp;V with a prominent capacity retention of 87.2% after 300 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0006">6d</a>). When the charge cut-off voltage is extended to 4.1&nbsp;V, the cell can display an extraordinary reversible capacity of 121.5&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> with capacity retention of 81.8% over 300 cycles. Even with high rate of 50 C, the test of long-term cycling performance over 3000 cycles in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0006">6e</a> reveals high capacities of 64.5 and 108.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> under 1.5–3.6 and 1.5–4.1&nbsp;V, with extraordinary capacity retentions of 60.5% and 63.5%, respectively. To fully grasp the superiority of the NVFP material, previously reported NASICON-based full devices are summarized in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0006">6f,g</a>, in which main electrochemical parameters, such as cell voltage, capacity, energy density, and power density, are included. Although the median voltage is relatively low, the HC//NVFP SIFCs can achieve an extremely high material-level energy density of about 304&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> due to activation of the V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> redox couple, which enables the transfer of 2.5e<sup>−</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0006">6f</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0005_R_d106366567e1929" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater. Small">5</a>, <a href="#adma202304428-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0011_R_d106366567e1929" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater. Carbon">11</a>, <a href="#adma202304428-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0013_R_d106366567e1929" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a>, <a href="#adma202304428-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0015_R_d106366567e1929" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Energy Lett.">15</a>, <a href="#adma202304428-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0017_R_d106366567e1929" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Storage Mater. Small">17</a>, <a href="#adma202304428-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0025_R_d106366567e1929" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">25</a>, <a href="#adma202304428-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0031_R_d106366567e1929" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nano Energy Small ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, HC//NVFP SIFCs show superiorities in both power density and energy density compared to the state-of-the-art reported SIFCs configurations (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304428-fig-0006">6g</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304428-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0005_R_d106366567e1938" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater. Small">5</a>, <a href="#adma202304428-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0015_R_d106366567e1938" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Energy Lett.">15</a>, <a href="#adma202304428-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0017_R_d106366567e1938" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Storage Mater. Small">17</a>, <a href="#adma202304428-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304428-bib-0031_R_d106366567e1938" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nano Energy Small ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202304428-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/37bd0743-215d-4d44-b18a-65b761ad0810/adma202304428-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/37bd0743-215d-4d44-b18a-65b761ad0810/adma202304428-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/96b34bf5-43b8-4b2d-bc71-927be36974ed/adma202304428-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/37bd0743-215d-4d44-b18a-65b761ad0810/adma202304428-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304428-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304428" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the HC//NVFP full cells. a) Charge–discharge profiles of NVFP cathode, HC anode and HC//NVFP full cells and schematic illustration of full cells (inset). b) Charge–discharge profiles and c) rate performance from 0.5 to 100 C. d,e) Long-term cycling stability at 10 C (d) and 50 C (e). f,g) Comparison of the voltage and capacity (f), as well as the energy density and power density (g) for the HC//NVFP full cell with other reported full cell configurations.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202304428-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202304428-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>A novel NASICON-type NVFP has been designed as a low-cost, high energy density and long-lasting cathode material for SIBs. The V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> redox couple is successfully activated at high-voltage plateaus by Fe doping. Initial discharge capacity of 110&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is provided in a voltage window of 2–3.8&nbsp;V. When the charge cut-off voltage is expanded to 4.3&nbsp;V, the capacity gains a respectable 148.2&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and the specific energy density reaches an enviable 501&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. The SIFCs fabricated by NVFP cathode and HC anode maintain the initial capacity of 63.5% after 3000 cycles at 50 C. And a material-level energy density of 304&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> can be achieved, which is comparable to or slightly better than that of other reported full cell configurations. It is confirmed by in situ XRD that the NVFP electrodes undergo reversible solid-solution and biphasic reaction mechanism upon Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion, and the lattice volume change is only 7.2%. Furthermore, Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>, V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup>, and V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> redox couples involved in charge transfer are also disclosed by ex situ XPS. DFT calculations further illustrate the favorable electronic structure and low Na<sup>+</sup> migration barriers of NVFP cathode. We also revealed that the unpaired electrons in the 3d orbital of Fe are inseparable for the activation of higher-valence redox reactions. Considering the low-cost doping and significant energy density, the NVFP cathode in our work further promotes the real applications of SIBs in the near future.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
